Date:2021/04/19 15:45:15 Country:CZE EU_CITIZEN ANONYMOUS

Dear Commission,

If the issue is the so called "hate speech", sure, some expressions, namely under some specific circumstances, could be considered as really harming and dishonesting another person. But there already exist laws to penalize them, as a verbal offence, as a defamation, as harassment etc. So there is no need for this inclusion, especially when it is so widely formulated. It would cantrarily lead to severe limitations of the right for expression, and so it would contradict the Universal Declaration of Human Rights, especially in the articles 18, 19 and 30. Who would consider whether an expression is already a hate or just a harmless joke? It could also impede someone to call things with right names,in some situations, and generally say the truth. This would be a dangerous intervention into democratic freedoms and basic human rights.

Thank you.

Michaela Bohatá

Date:2021/04/19 15:33:25 Country:DEU NGO ANONYMOUS

TERRE DES FEMMES – Menschenrechte für die Frau e.V. strongly welcomes this Commission initiative to extend the list of EU crimes to all forms of hate crime and hate speech. This initiative will strengthen the efforts to prevent and combat gender-based violence on- and offline. As the largest women’s rights organisation in Germany, the focus of our work is the fight against all forms of violence against girls and women. We are strongly aware of the consequences of gender-based hate speech, as it opens the floodgates to physical abuse and violence. With our campaign #UNHATEWOMEN, we have been raising awareness of the dangers of hate speech glorifying sexual violence in song lyrics and on social media. Hate speech leads to social acceptance of verbal violence and lowers the inhibiting threshold for physical abuse. As it can be seen through the reaction to our campaign, this issue is more relevant than ever: it culminated in the rapper Fler, who is known for his misogynist lyrics, suspending bounty for a female social media user who called him out for his hateful speech. This illustrates how closely linked gender-based hate speech is to physical danger.
In order to ensure a more effective protection against hate speech and hate crime, we strongly urge the Commission to inclusion gender as a motive. The quantity and quality of digital violence experienced by women differs decidedly from what heterosexual white men experience online. It is not only deeply personal, but in most cases also highly sexualised and predominantly misogynistic, with women getting attacked “simply” because of their gender. Women receive threats of rape; they are insulted as being sexually frustrated or simply ordered "back to the kitchen". It can be observed that various platforms and social media, the achievements of gender equality are questioned as a matter of course.
Coordinated action at EU level is urgently needed to ensure adequate protection of women’s right to live a violence-free life. The first step must be to include hate speech and hate crime in the list of EU crimes. Hate speech and hate crime are cross-border crimes, especially when committed online. Further, they are crimes of particular seriousness because they firstly play a major role in mass crimes and secondly, because they have lasting impact on the victims.
Misogyny acts as a gateway to right-wing extremism and different ideologies which reject gender equality as a fundamental part of modern society. This is illustrated by the horrific attacks in Halle (Germany), and Christchurch (New Zealand). All of these horrid attacks were perpetrated by individuals who adhered not only anti-semitic worldviews, but also their had the hatred towards women in common. All perpetrators expressed their views through gender-based hate speech on social media, music, and other ways.
Women and girls are the largest and most affected group by hate speech and digital violence (FRA “Violence against women: an EU-wide survey”). A study concludes that 88 % of internet users in Germany having ever witnessed digital violence stated that the observed hatred was directed at women. These rates come close to the rates of hate speech against refugees and exceed anti-Semitic hate speech (BKA “Politisch motivierte Kriminalität 2019”). According to Plan International (2020), 58% of young women have been abused online. 19% reduced their social media use after being affected. Hate speech can provenly cause mental suffering such as depression, panic attacks and sleeping disorder. Consistent intimidation of women online puts their safety and their freedom of expression at risk. When no action is taken against hate speech, there is real danger of the silent effect of female activist, politicians, journalist etc and/or the withdrawal of women from the digital and political sphere. In order to tackle this form of violence, we strongly call on the Commission to recognise gender-based hate speech and hate crime as an EU crime.

Tomas Kocanek

Date:2021/04/19 14:55:22 Country:CZE EU_CITIZEN WITHINFO

Postih za nenávistnou řeč jako cesta k lepší společnosti je iluzí. Nefunguje to, dokonce ani v případě, že by se podařilo dokonale vymezit, co to přesně je nenávistná řeč. Budeme ještě schopni s takovým vymezením popsat realisticky stav věcí ? Vždyť potřebujeme, více než kdy jindy, otevřenou komunikaci. Lživý popis reality nemůže společnost přivést k účinnému řešení problémů našeho života. Pravdivý popis stavu je základem každého dobrého řešení se šancí na úspěch. Je zkušeností nejen poslední doby, že právě snaha o pravdivý popis naší reality je vykládán jako nenávistná řeč, čímž je fakticky blokována cesta k řešení problému. Pravdivý popis je nahrazen politicky korektním, ale nefunkčním, přijatá řešení se míjí účinkem.
Chcete mít nově pravomoc k výkladu lidských vyjádření, zda nejsou nenávistná. Některá jistě jsou. Ale cestou zlepšení situace není postih, cestou je komunikace, empatie, sounáležitost. Vždyť řeč je nám dána pro možnost vyjádření sama sebe. Je součástí služby bližnímu jej vyslechnout tak jak právě cítí a je-li jeho řeč skutečně nenávistná, ptát se přátelsky po jeho životních cestách, které jej k nenávisti přivedly. S nenávistí se zcela jistě nerodíme.
Slovo má svou sílu a lidé by měli ve vlastním zájmu, každý sám za sebe, tuto sílu pochopit. Cestou pochopení této síly je však vzdělání, komunikace, vnímání. Co do této cesty pochopení vnášejí tresty? Jen nový problém, jen další překážku pro vzájemné porozumění mezi lidmi.
Ve společnosti, která chápe tyto souvislosti, resp. vedení té společnosti to chápe, je ustanovena svoboda slova, aby každý člen společnosti mohl svým vyjádřením otevřeně přispět k celkovému vývoji. Tím je umožněno využití lidského potenciálu celé společnosti. Bez svobody slova je slyšet jen úzkou skupinu lidí, jež je právě obdařena mocí. Jsme toho bohužel každodenními svědky.
Chcete dnes mít pravomoc postihovat člověka za jeho vyjádření. Lidé u moci, kteří by chtěli sílu svěřené moci používat pro organizaci harmonického rozvoje společnosti, takoví lidé by nikdy podobnou pravomocí nechtěli disponovat. Vy ji chcete, a už toto vypovídá zcela jasně, že výše uvedené souvislosti vůbec nechápete. Neexistuje smysluplné využití takové pravomoci, vždy, když něco takového použijete, vždy to bude jen nově vložená překážka pro vzájemné pochopení. Je šance, abyste to pochopili už při zrodu této iniciativy? Nebo alespoň teď?
Prosím, zastavte tuto snahu o novou pravomoc. Pojďme společně pracovat na tom, aby lidé díky svým spokojeným životů nemuseli žádnou nenávistnou řeč používat. Pojďme se upřímně ptát, co jim v té spokojenosti brání. Role vedení společnosti je udržet její harmonii, a řeč, třeba i nenávistná, je indikátorem stavu. Je mnohem těžší pracovat s nelichotivým svědectvím o realitě než jen prostě a lacině zastavit taková lidská svědectví. Svou vůdčí odpovědnost nemůžete naplňovat tak, že zastavíte tok špatných zpráv! Vaše odpovědnost je v pečlivém vnímání reálného stavu a hledání realistických cest nápravy. Zastavení špatných zpráv je cestou iluze, zkratka do pekla, a takovou cestu musí dnes každý soudný člověk odmítnout. Musíme pracovat s realitou, s otevřenými vyjádřeními lidí. Jste v mocenských pozicích a máte plnou odpovědnost za to, jakou cestu budete chtít vedeným lidem prostřednictvím zákonů a nařízení diktovat.
Mám naději, že naznačené souvislosti pochopíte a budete svou moc používat pro harmonický život společnosti. Tresty za vyjadřování vedou k iluzi harmonie, kterou pak většina lidí není schopna sdílet a prožívat. Protože skutečné prožívání není možné bez svobodného vyjadřování.
Prosím, zastavte toto destruktivní zneužití moci, stalo by se to jen zdrojem nového utrpení lidí pod vaším vedením.

Date:2021/04/19 13:08:35 Country:IRL EU_CITIZEN WITHINFO

I am deeply concerned at the proposal to include hate speech on the list of EU crimes. I think if this goes ahead it will do much harm.

1. Hate Speech laws undermine democracy.
As Nadine Strossen, ex-President of the ACLU, explained in her book "Hate: Why We Should Resist It With Free Speech, Not Censorship",
freedom of speech is essential in a democracy because it ensures that the government does not become the arbiter of the acceptable viewpoint at any given time. Strossen points out how vital this principle has been in defending not just what society might now classify as ‘hate speech’ such as racism but also pro-civil-rights messages. Strossen cites the 1972 Chicago v Mosley case, in which an African-American postal employee was only allowed to protest outside a high school, with a sign that read ‘Jones High School practices black discrimination’, on the basis of the First Amendment.

2. Hate Speech laws undermine the rights of minorities.
Strossen goes on to show how hate-speech laws can often end up targeting the very minority groups such laws purport to protect. Examples include that of a prominent journalist and member of the Uzbek minority in Kyrgyzstan who was charged with ‘inciting ethnic hatred’ for reporting on conflicts between Uzbeks and the majority Kygryz.

3. Hate speech laws can be in practice, Blasphemy Laws, putting ideas and beliefs beyond criticism.
We can see the likelihood of this happening if we look at cases like that of Maya Forstater, whose lack of belief in innate gender identity and her belief that biological sex is real was pronounced a belief unworthy of respect in a civilised society.

4. Hate Speech laws can be used cynically for political purpose.
As the ex-CEO of Index on Censorship, Jodie Ginsberg, said in 2016:
"You need look no further than the discussions within the same Human Rights Council for evidence of the way in which hate speech laws are easily manipulated to target those with opposing view rather than protect minorities. During the debate, Russia praised hate speech laws, talking of the need to monitor “hate speech“ in Ukraine so as not to ignite “nationalistic fires.”

5. Hate Speech laws can exacerbate political polarisation.
Currently politics is becoming more polarised. If we want to reverse this trend, we must be able to have difficult conversations. Hate Speech laws have a chilling effect on many vital discussions. As the US delegation noted in a Human Rights Council meeting in 2016: “legal prohibitions on incitement are often used to persecute members of minority groups and political opponents, raising serious freedom of expression concerns.

“Such laws, including blasphemy laws, tend to reinforce divisions rather than promote societal harmony. The presence of these laws has little discernable effect on reducing actual incidences of hate speech. In some cases such laws actually serve to foment violence against members of minority groups accused of expressing unpopular viewpoints.

“In addition, legal prohibitions can displace societal efforts to combat intolerance. This occurs because disputes over hate speech are then seen as matters for courts to decide rather than society at large. Combating hate speech requires a change in the societal attitudes that give rise to discriminatory views. Prohibiting speech is a poor, if not counterproductive, means of achieving that goal.”

I ask that you rethink this proposal and recommit to the principle of robust protection for freedom of speech.

Derek Haas

Date:2021/04/19 12:07:58 Country:CZE EU_CITIZEN WITHINFO

I strongly oppose the proposal and find its reasoning to be very vague and unconvincing. The issues of "hate speech" and "hate crime" are very sensitive in nature, because mainly the concept of "hate speech" means collision between the basic human right to be protected from harm and basic human right of free speech. Such balancing between these two rights shall not be done on EU level (but on the national level instead), given the differences in constitutional background and criminal laws in member countries. "Steamrolling" these into "EU crimes" is not a solution and can do more harm than gain. Also the claim that hate speech and hate crime "often cross borders" is not really a viable reasoning, because in globalised society this can be said about almost every criminal activity. Lastly, the proposed solution ignores the fact that member states have already implemented legal measures aimed at fighting hate speech and hate crime; even if these measures would have been found to be ineffective (which is contrary to my opinion), the remedy should be done primarily by the member states, as this is the leading principle applied in other areas, which are possibly just not so trendy as the one in question.

Alix LEJARD

Date:2021/04/19 10:14:03 Country:FRA NGO WITHINFO

Est-il juste d’inclure le crime de discours de haine et de crime de haine dans la législation de l’union européenne Article 83(1) TFEU ?

Dans le cadre de cette consultation publique, Femina Europa souligne les points suivants:

Sachant que la loi doit être juste c’est à dire être au service du bien commun et non au service de ceux qui l’édictent.

- Il y a un caractère disproportionné à inclure le «discours de haine» au même niveau que les autres crimes graves mentionnés à l'article 83 (1) TFUE - tels que le terrorisme, la traite des êtres humains, l'exploitation sexuelle des femmes et des enfants, le trafic de drogues illicites, d'armes et la criminalité organisée.

- A quel titre le «discours de haine» peut il est assimilé à un «crime grave»? - Il n’est pas nécessaire d’inclure le «discours de haine» au niveau des crimes de l’UE, car la plupart des pays de l’UE ont déjà une législation jugeant et pénalisant les «discours de haine», dans leur propre système juridique.

- La plupart des pays de l’UE réservent aux discours de haine des peines pénales beaucoup plus faibles et en rien comparable aux peines pour trafic de drogue, d’armes et trafic d’êtres humains.

-Le droit fondamental à la liberté d’expression est menacé par la criminalisation du discours de haine. En France ce droit est déjà borné d’un côté par la loi sur la presse du 29 juillet 1881 qui interdit la censure et de l’autre par la loi du 13 juillet 1990 qui pénalise le négationisme.

- Ajouter les notions de sexe, d’orientation sexuelle, de l’âge et du handicap implique que certaines catégories d’ individus n’auraient pas la même valeur que d’autres.

-La haine est un ressenti subjectif qui dépend de l'expérience personnelle, et qui peut reposer sur des erreurs d’interprétation. La loi juge les actes et non les sentiments.

Pour toutes ces raisons nous pensons que le discours de haine et le crime de haine ne peuvent être mis sur le même plan.

Criminaliser une opinion c’est entériner l’intolérance et favoriser la violence. Protéger la liberté d’expression c’est nourrir le dialogue et la tolérance.

Femina Europa

Michal Rybka

Date:2021/04/19 04:55:02 Country:CZE EU_CITIZEN WITHINFO

Freedom of Speech is essential to any free society. Even today the "Freedom of expression" is severely limited according to The Human Rights Act Article 10: Freedom of expression - there is a lot of exceptions limiting free speech. Actually there are so many of them that is hard to say we have "right to express ourselves", it is more "right to stay silent and keep our ideas for ourselves". We do not need any more restrictions, especially these that are very subjective and can be abused as a weapon against the freedoms of others. Seriously - what is the difference between "hate speech against the religion" and "blasphemy laws"? Anyone who does not agree with someone else can be blamed as "hateful". What about people who reject "gender-neutral language" on religious principles? What about secularists and atheists who do reject religion completely? What about religious texts itselves - read The Bible or Quran, it is full of "hate speech". Are you going to ban these books? Freedom of speech guarantees that anyone can say anything according their point of view and cannot be prosecuted for that. It does not solve all the problems, it just guarantees the law cannot be weaponised against those who do not agree with others. We need more freedom of speech, not any more restrictions!

Zdeněk Svoboda

Date:2021/04/19 03:39:36 Country:CZE EU_CITIZEN WITHINFO

Dear All, The trouble with your legislation draft is that it might be difficult to distinguish and define what “hate” speech really is. As other comments here correctly suggest, you can label as “hate” almost everything what suits you. Either disagreement, or criticism, revealing the truth, quoting the facts etc. Therefore, all this could easily become very effective tool curtailing the freedom of the speech and intimidating anybody who could have even slightly different views than “official” and “approved” guidelines.
How do you want to distinguish yourself from Russia and China when you bringing in such a legislation? I spent my younghood in communist Czechoslovakia and remember very well how anything other than total agreement with gov. and communist propaganda could be labelled as “hate against our ally - the Soviet Union and socialistic society”. The consequences might have been expelling from the school, job loss, court proceeding, imprisonment, bullying from the side of secret police etc. Is this what you really want? Not everything can, or should, be achieved through courts, prosecution, police, informants, censorship, etc. This is not the way how anybody can with the battle of ideas and opinions. Best regards, Zdeněk Svoboda, Prague.

I find this idea absolutely unacceptable at least for the following two reasons (in short):

1. Expanding the list of EU crimes with hate speech and hate crime can easily lead to legal arbitrariness.
If “hate speech” term is not narrowly define, anyone can potentially be brought to justice for what he has said (for anything). So it can easily become an instrument of harassment.
But if the term is defined in details, it would be the creation of the Orwell’s Newspeak! Any freedom would be put under risk- it would be just matter of time! The both scenarios are catastrophic!

2. Such expansion is very controversial: it aims to fight the discrimination, but it is discrimination by itself! If there was a violation of the law, it shouldn’t be treated explicitly, with advantage toward similar violations! The communists enjoyed to say: “The end justifies the means”, but is it so? Will we be better than the “lawbreakers” then?

Martin Hans Jomstein

Date:2021/04/19 00:33:13 Country:DEU EU_CITIZEN WITHINFO

As a German citizen I still remember our nation's dark history. That history was precluded with, among other things, heavy censorship and prosecution based on opinion and outcome, completely ignoring the origins and intent of said "crime".

I find any regulation of Free speech absolutely unacceptable. In this case, specifically, I find it extremely dangerous to the whole EU. If we, as United Europe, want to remain free, we need to defend freedom. That includes Freedom of Speech as a basic concept of a free society.

The problem with this effort can be seen on multiple levels but the most prominent issue is with "who would define what is 'hate speech' and 'hate crime' " - these are way too subjective values and setting them basically arbitrarily and absolutely is in direct conflict with freedom. Setting them specifically must in any case result in a state that is unacceptable in any law-governing environment: given the way this material is presented, it is obvious that you want to overstep basic law principles. The most prominent of those principles is prosecution based on outcome rather than intent. Prosecution based on outcome has no place in any legal framework that calls itself "rule of law", "freedom" or similar. Prosecution based on outcome is basically a totalitarian approach which allows to prosecute literally anyone based on literally anything.

Such laws have no place in a free society. Please stop this effort. Please don't regulate Free speech - it's not Free speech when someone cannot voice their opinion because they're afraid that someone else might consider them "hate speech". "Hate" speech and "hate" crime simply cannot be defined in a way that doesn't effectively become a witch hunt.

I was always pro-EU but some of the latest moves, including this one, make me think that EU is on a (slow) way to become a similar political force as the Third Reich or the USSR. And as a citizen with both Third Reich and USSR history, I'm very, very afraid of this, scared even.

Lukas Harta

Date:2021/04/18 22:10:26 Country:DEU EU_CITIZEN WITHINFO

The idea to make hate speech and hate crime “EU crimes” according to Art. 83 TFEU is problematic for several reasons. First, these concepts are notoriously difficult to define. The Commission does not suggest a definition nor would, in all likelihood, one be included in the text of Art. 83 TFEU. This is particularly problematic for hate speech since provisions that criminalise certain forms of speech by definition interfere with a fundamental right, namely freedom of expression (Art. 11 CFR). In that regard, hate speech is fundamentally different from all the EU crimes already included in Art. 83 TFEU. As the ECtHR has put it in Handyside, freedom of expression also includes the right to offend, shock and disturb. Any interference with that right must be provided for by law (Art. 52 para. 1 CFR), which entails that the legal basis must be sufficiently clear and precise. Whether a provision targeting hate speech can fulfil that is doubtful. In any case, there is a clear risk of self-censorship, i.e. that people would refrain from exercising their freedom of expression because the are unsure about the limits of “hate speech”.

Second, I cannot see how hate crime and hate speech as such could fall under the category of “particularly serious crime with a cross-border dimension”. While hate speech can, of course, have a cross-border dimension by virtue of being carried out online, this is by no means always the case. Verbal racist slurs on the street on the other hand, as offensive and hurtful as they can be, do not have a cross-border dimension, neither from the nature or impact of such offences nor from a special need to combat them on a common basis. The same is even more the case for other acts of hate crime which cannot even be carried out online. Likewise, it is more than doubtful whether acts of hate speech and hate crime per se constitute particularly serious crime. It is possible, of course, as most painfully visible from acts like the murder of Walter Lübcke because of his refugee-welcoming political attitude. But I don’t believe that e.g. an act causing minor damage to property becomes an act of particularly serious crime solely because of the motive behind the act. The harm to society that such acts cause is significantly lower than that caused by existing EU crimes such as human trafficking or counterfeiting of money.

Marcela BROTHÁNKOVÁ

Date:2021/04/18 22:09:45 Country:CZE EU_CITIZEN WITHINFO

Martin Hýsek

Date:2021/04/18 21:51:01 Country:CZE EU_CITIZEN WITHINFO

I am fundamentally opposed to any attempts of curbing the free speech, even for seemingly worthy reasons such as protection of marginalized groups. The devil, as always, lies in the details, namely, the definitions. Hate speech is defined as form of expression intend to vilify, humiliate, or incite hatred against certain group. That is very vague. Any negative comment can be made to fit such a definition. Especially if the group is disproportionately represented in crime statistics etc.
Generally speaking we might agree we should have right to criticize anyone, or any group. But by which criteria should we differentiate between mere criticism and hate speech? Can you define such objective criteria?
Yes, it is easy to think of some clear-cut examples of each, but there is a broad gray area, when the subsumption is difficult and necessarily subjective. There is a legal principle, nulla poena sine lege certa, no penalty without a certain law. Vaguely defined crimes are a deathblow to a freedom – for example famous Article 58 in USSR penal code. Democratic states that respect the rule of law cannot get even close to use such a vague terms in its criminal law. Yes, you will always have some small gray area, but it cannot be too broad. If we don’t know by which behaviour we will actually break the law, we have no legal certainty, which is cornerstone of the rule of law. Freedom of speech is there to protect controversial, even ugly speech. You can’t legitimately have a situation, when on one hand the law protects your controversial speech, and on the other hand you will be prosecuted for crossing the line of acceptable speech, when you don’t really know where the line is set (and the line is possibly very broad). Advice “if you’re not sure, then don’t say it” is not acceptable to me, if the words “freedom of speech” are to have any meaning at all.
In my mind, the term “hate speech” is somewhat comparable to “non-constructive criticism”. Can we deny that some empty, futile complaining that deserves to be called “non-constructive criticism”, can and does exist? Of course not. But the term is so vague, that almost any form of undesirable criticism can be cast aside as “non-constructive”. The danger of rejecting necessary, healthy criticism on those ground is so high, that banning “non-constructive” criticism should not be allowed.
To give the state broad opportunity to prosecute “hate speech” defined as such is to make sure the power will be abused eventually. That which can be easily abused, will be abused, that is ever valid political axiom. It can be easily used to stifle public debate about certain minorities. To ordinary person, even a mere prosecution is a punishment in itself, even if it results in exoneration. It is a public warning to everyone, who is thinking about engaging in controversial debates like that: “Do you want to have such problems? No? So shut up.” The state shouldn’t have such a power to stifle public debate like that.
My question to all of you who are prone to support this initiative: Can you honestly define hate speech in such terms as to avoid all the problems I have described? And don’t tell me it is for the lawyers. Most people are not lawyers. (I am a lawyer, and I still don’t really know how to define hate speech in such a way that clearly differentiates it from “normal” criticism.) We have a principle "ignorance of the law excuses not”. At least when it comes to criminal law, it should be strongly adhered to. The main purpose for this principle is to avoid absurd defenses like “but Your Honor, I didn’t know it is illegal to rape and murder.” Knowing the law must mean knowing what I am and am not allowed to do. Ordinary people shouldn’t need to have a lawyer to know what is and is not a crime. And when it comes to the hate speech laws, that is exactly what they need. Without a lawyer, and possible even with him, they cannot be sure, what they are and are not allowed to say. That is not acceptable.

Tato iniciativa vnáší do trestního práva politický a ideologický prvek, což bude oním pověstným krokem přes řeku Rubikon, po němž již nebude cesty zpět. Pro úplnost dodávám, že ostře protestuji mezi zařazení skutkové podstaty nenávistného slovního projevu mezi zvlášť závažné trestné činy. Jejich společenská nebezpečnost ani náznakem nedosahuje této trestně-právní kategorie. Jsem bytostně přesvědčen, že v případě schválení na půdě Evropské komise se tato směrnice střetne s obrovským odporem v mnoha členských státech a výrazně přispěje k dalšímu rozkolu v rámci již tak závažně narušených vztahů v EU.

Ladislav Holík

Date:2021/04/18 20:29:08 Country:CZE EU_CITIZEN WITHINFO

As an EU citizen with background in law enforcement I am deeply concerned over the new proposal to include hate speech among EU crimes. The proposed crime, which could be only vaguely defined and very easily politically abused, should have no place among real serious crimes such as money laundering, drugs trafficking, environmental crime, intellectual property crime or organized crime. It is in interest of all EU states to fight crime. But there have already been attempts to use hate speech as a tool to infringe the fundamental right of free speech or to silence those with dissent ideas. Freedom of speech, freedom of conscience, freedom of thoughts have been the core values of Western democracies since the 18th century. In my country freedom of speech used to be infringed by the undemocratis regime that treated dissent ideas as a crime. Do not let come this legislation in action, since democratic European states should never treat the "Andersdenkenden" as criminals.

Vůči jedněm nenávist povinná, vůči druhým nežádoucí - ale tolerovaná. Klidně jste mohl kašlat na téze x-tého sjezdu KSSS či na závěry předsednictva KSČ. Filip Vybíhal

Date:2021/04/18 19:15:39 Country:CZE EU_CITIZEN WITHINFO

In my opinion, there are several issues with this proposal:

First and foremost is that this proposal, by criminalizing hate speech, violates the 19th Article of The Universal Declaration of Human Rights. Hate speech is also based on the perception of the “victim” or the one judging, and is similar to the concept of blasphemy. It's validity cannot be observed by no one in particular, which is in direct contradiction with the values and ideals of liberal science and liberal society.

Second is that it further undermines the sovereignty of EU Member states to decide its own laws.

Third is that hate crimes are a dubious concept. If someone catches their partner cheating and kills them, isn’t that motivated by hate? If someone attacks a member of an opposing political party, isn’t that motivated by hate? What makes one hate more worthy of attention than another?

And finally, some of the data to support this proposal is in-complete, inconclusive and even contradictory.
By the author’s own admission, there is no proof that hate speech and hate crimes have a serious impact or that persecuting them helps with decreasing hate.
The reports on sexism either don't actually examine the experiences of both sexes and compare them with each other or if they do, the authors of this proposal choose to ignore those comparisons. Therefore they can’t prove that the cause of what is described is sexism and can’t exclude that other factors are the cause, such as MPs being public figures in case of the survey by the IPU. More complete studies, like one from Pew Research or even one by U-Report linked in this proposal, found that on the internet, men and women experience the same amount of harassment.
The reports on anti-semitism suggest, based on the timeline provided, countries studied and self reporting by the victims, that members of a minority group covered by this proposal are in large part responsible for the increase in anti-semitism. Addressing the issue of anti-semitism effectively would therefore result in a violation of this proposal.

Peter Šutek

Jakub Sedlacek

Date:2021/04/18 17:59:26 Country:CZE EU_CITIZEN WITHINFO

Such initiative cannot be taken on EU-wide level and must be left in the competency of each member state.

Understanding of what can be construed as "hateful" may be more difficult than one can imagine. Whereas in one part of one country a term may be particularly offensive or downright hateful, it may carry a completely different meaning in its neighboring country.

Many countries, particularly in areas such as the Balkans use languages that are so close in their form, yet may carry absolutely different meanings or sense for the exact same sentence. Same applies for countries like Poland and Czech Republic, where a single word may have a different meaning completely.

Having an interconnected union and having active social communication channels could mean that a big can of worms would be opened in the legal understanding and interpretations that lead to places where the European justice does not wish to find itself in.

In addition, as places such as the United States or our former member the United Kingdom have shown, the varying hate speech specifities of people's who live just miles away from one another caused fiery confrontations that bury the legislative branch of the US under an effective deadlock, while in the UK, more specifically in Scotland it created an extremely hostile and fearful environments for artists and political activists alike. As commented on by figures such as Ricky Gervais, who's art is now exposing them to an unnecessary levels of potential legal issues - a scenario so insane that it should most certainly not be replicated.

What is even worse - certain areas that could be under very specific edge scenarios construed as hateful could provide a large basis for fraudulent or frivolous lawsuits that would most certainly have dire effect on public political discourse, starting the Europe down the dangerous route of potential future of political extremism, which easily fester in the darkness of the legal uncertainty that is any hate speech law in practice. This is caused always by the simple fact that regulation of certain speech can never be absolutely defined in clear and predictive manner, which was already proven in the English speaking sphere of he world. An effort being even more impossible when applied over a union, members of which speak in more than 20 languages.

Vážení,
1/ kriminalizovat verbální projevy je cestou jak umlčovat oponenty
2/ kdo chce chránit před slovy jiných, nebude mít nakonec ani ochranu, ani svobodu
3/ kdo se cítí poškozen či je mu poškozena újma, ať fyzicky, virtuálně (nehmotně) či verbláně, má právo na občanskoprávní ochranu.
4/ odmítám komise, které budou posuzovat hranici mezi humorem, ironií, satirou, názorem a Vámi navrhovanými trestnými činy
5/ kriminalizace Vámi navržených jednání by znamenala podporu skrývání vlastních názorů na veřejnosti, stejně jak jsme již zažili za komunismu.
6/ kriminalizace a veřejná diskriminace lidí, kteří mají vlastní názor, odlišný od Vámi prosazované korektnosti by vedla k rozdělení společnosti napříč různými, Vámi definovanými skupinami
Proto jednoznačně odmítám verbální trestné činy nad rámec stávajícíl legislativy, zejména §181 Trest. zákona (Uvedení v omyl) a §184 Trest zákona (Pomluva).
S pozdravem,

Date:2021/04/18 13:53:34 Country:CZE EU_CITIZEN ANONYMOUS

I must strongly oppose this notion of adding hate speach to the list of EU crimes and for EC to decide the extent of criminal law of member states. EU should first and foremost adhere to being an economic association of states not a political organization dabbling in criminal affairs. Furthermore I believe free speech to be the basis of modern society. As history has shown both in times of monarchies of the past and in totalitarian regimes of the 20th century, using vague frameworks for criminal law such as "anti-state behavior" will create only terror and tyranny. Such would be the fate of "hate-speach" as we can see for example in the recent incident in soccer where the Czech based player was punished with a 10 match stop based on (what's worse - unproven) hate-speach while a player who has violently kicked another in the head causing severe injury was punished with just a 4 match stop. This new trend where words are viewed as worse than physical harm is a dangerous one and must not be pursued.
To summarize - I believe that member states already have good enough framework of laws which can be used to punish all truly harmful behavior. Anything else then physical violence or incitement to violence should be protected as free speech under constitution.

They simply smoke around my bedroom and therefore I can't get fresh air while I sleep. If I say something, let's say, I say, smokers are killing my sleep, I will be punished? How about punish smoker that he is smoking my bedroom out? My English is not that good to explain what I mean, but we should teach humans to take insults "one ear in and another out". It is the same with trolls. if you feed them (this law will feed them), they will grow. if you ignore them, (not block, but IGNORE), they will starve out and no one will take them seriosly. I've got lot of friends from lot of races and colours, never had problem with anyone, but when someone pisses me off (smoker or some dangeros driver, etc, etc), I want to have the right to tell what my opinion is.
Also, last but not least, each and every opinion push us forward. If only one listener will understand the opinion he or she can simply make the best out of it, and help community or anyone in or outside of the "hate speech" opinion.
I think the more free speech, the better for anyone, since only true open talk can solve any issues or problems (just like in a marriage), we simply NEED honest opinions, otherwise it's going to be a huge problem in a decade or so. imagine - everyone will be afraid to tell the truth, and everyone will think there are no issues, and then one day, boom, the issue explodes (hnger, ICE motors, green energy, whatever)
Just thnk about it please.
Hava a nice day and thank you.

Jan Matěj

Date:2021/04/18 12:29:20 Country:CZE EU_CITIZEN WITHINFO

This is unacceptable.
We don't need legislation to protect people from mean or ugly words unless they rise to the level of threats, direct incitement to crime, or defamation. What we need more of is legislative to protect free speech.
Freedom of speech is the most basic human right. It's also the foundation of our civil society. It allows us to resolve conflicts and disputes peacefully. It allows us to even let others know there are issues that need resolving, which may not be obvious at the first glance. (One look at the grooming gangs problem of our former member UK gives you more example of the need for what many consider "hate speech" than most people can stomach)
The right not to be offended is not a human right and that's for a good reason. Offence is very subjective and almost intrinsic to strong disagreement on any issue where both sides are offended by each other's positions. Historically whenever there were attempts to protect people's sensibilities the censorship never stopped at senseless vulgarity but was immediately used to censor art, valid criticism, and opposing arguments.
What good does it to force people with extreme views to go underground and self radicalize or radicalize in some small echo chamber? Let people speak, then you'll know what they are and whether they are a danger to someone.
"Hate speech" and hate crime are in no way equivalent. There might be a good reason to treat violent crime motivated by blind hatred differently from other violent crimes of equivalent severity, but we cannot allow this to be mixed up with increasingly totalitarian tendencies on the ground of expression.

Jan Svoboda

Date:2021/04/18 09:32:25 Country:CZE EU_CITIZEN WITHINFO

Hate speech and hate crime are concepts that often invoke a subjective interpretation and hard to categorically define.

Hate is an emotion and therefore each speech and action require interpretation in order to be classified hate speech or hate crime.

This potentially opens doors to arbitrary application of law. In order to prevent this, the definition would need to be very strict and robust against loose interpretation.
Hate speech should be limited to incitement of violence and incitement of limiting rights and freedoms. It must not allow criminalization of criticism, whether correct, incorrect or offending some party.
Lesser crimes like libels should be regulated on national level, where local customs and culture of speech may be taken into account.

Serious debates often include criticism of actions or values. Such actions or values can be correlated or otherwise associated with groups of people. Interpreting speech as offensive or potentially offensive to someone must not be the determining factor of hate speech. Such interpretations are subjective and it is not fully in the author's control whether someone takes offense after listening to his speech. The definition must prevent weaponizing law towards political or other debate of contested and even controversial topics. Such debates are necessary part of social and political discourse and being able to express one's concerns freely is among the highest principles of European tradition and values, even if harshly questioning our values.

The applicability should not be specific dimensions (race, sex, religion,...). Criminal law should be applied generally and not only choose to protect specific kinds of groups of people, otherwise it would create privileged (in original meaning of the word) strata in society. Such create animosities against the privileged and undermine stability. What difference is animosity due to religious conscience versus animosity due to political conscience or hate due to social class?
In the end, the goal should be to protect ANY individual against unsolicited violence or discriminatory violation of freedoms, not just chosen minorities.

Ultimately, it is questionable, whether hate crime should be a type of a crime on it's own. In my view and understanding of legal systems, motive is something that if proven can lead to harsher penalty under the original crime definition. Same crime done due to confusion under extreme circumstance is usually treated differently than same crime being premediated and with malicious intent.
Implementing a different class of crime may inadvertently impact the legal traditions in member states. Where national implementation may offer judge a more nuanced options in treating specific crime within range of punishment, border cases of determining 'regular' vs 'hate' crime might bring a big step in punishment due to hairsplitting difference in categorization.

Recent developments in USA where concepts such as 'microaggressions' etc. are being regularly weaponized as a means to censor opposing political or ethical views, even if not legally, rise concerns for freedom of speech. It should be needless to say, that when people feel threatened for their right of free expression, tensions may grow ultimately undoing any positive goals and threaten the stability of countries.

Peter Kovalčík

Jan Duben

Date:2021/04/17 20:56:50 Country:CZE EU_CITIZEN WITHINFO

I, like many others, think that this is a very bad idea. In order to understand my opposition to it, ask yourself: What is the purpose of freedom of speech? What does free speech protect?

Does it protect popular/societally accepted speech? Why would it? What needs to be protected? Most people already approve of such speech.

It really serves to protect unpopular/controversial speech. Without freedom of speech, society cannot improve, because any attempts at addressing any preceived wrong-doing/injustice/controversy will be punished, instead of sparking a debate. And like that, society either becomes stagnant, or resorts to violence.

It may seem trivial, but if a trivial thing such as calling someone a mean word can be criminalized, then anything else more severe can be criminalized as well. There's no limiting principle.

This legislation would be a direct violation of one of the most fundamental rights the entire Western civilization is founded on – a person's right to express their opinion without governmental persecution.

Tendai Mukau

Date:2021/04/17 18:20:54 Country:DEU NON_EU_CITIZEN WITHINFO

As a lawyer who extensively studies issues of fundamental rights in the European human rights (ECHR) system and the EU system (and future EU citizen), I wish to lodge my most strenuous objections to this, perhaps well-intentioned, but dangerous legislative project. Restrictions on speech, especially on the basis of impermissibly vague and subjective notions of "hate speech" and the like, are measures which are not "necessary in a democratic society," nor for the preservation of order and peace, nor for the protection of human dignity and life. A sufficient legal framework to protect individual rights already exists in Member State criminal law. To the extent individuals are actually harmed by hate-motivated abuse or violence, this is a matter of inadequate enforcement of existing criminal statutes which forbid harassment, physical attacks, criminal assault, discrimination etc. This proposed initiative does not protect fundamental rights, but rather further limits them. Restrictions on speech frequently (as evinced by several pending criminal trials in various Member States and recent and pending ECtHR case law), are utilized by governments to suppress unpopular or controversial views, and moreover the ability to critique religious dogma and practices, even on human rights grounds. This curtailment of debate and speech is intolerable in a free society and the ability to speak freely on controversial topics, safe from governmental criminal sanction, is a necessary precondition in achieving social progress and human rights. If anything, as evinced by numerous recent violent attacks against critics of religion in the EU, EU citizens need sharper protections safeguarding their fundamental right to expression, not further attacks upon them. Finally, the EU is acting ultra vires of its core mandate here. The EU exists to promote the functioning of the Single Market and the representation of the interests of the Member States collectively. Interfering in the criminal law of Member States is an undue intrusion into state sovereignty and one which is not at all necessary for the furtherance of the EU's mandate.

Miloš Sivák

Date:2021/04/17 18:01:18 Country:CZE EU_CITIZEN WITHINFO

Vážené dámy, vážení pánové.

Dovoluji si zaslat svůj názor na zařazení verbálních trestných činů týkajících se nenávistných projevů pod článek 83 Smlouvy o Evropské Unii (dále jen EU).
Rozdělím jej do několika bodů:
1) Domnívám se, že svoboda projevu (stejně jako např. svobody vyznání, atd.) je naprosto základní hodnotou nadřazenou běžným právům občana.
2) Verbální projevy, jakkoli mnohdy nevhodné, hloupé nebo dokonce nechutné, jsou ve své nebezpečnosti řádově níže než skutkové trestné činy jako jsou nejrůznější projevy násilí ať už beze zbraně nebo s jakoukoli zbraní.
3) Umlčování jakéhokoli hlasu a jakéhokoli názoru, byť s ním třeba bytostně nesouhlasíme, je vždy škodlivé. Výjimkou jsou snad jen projevy zpochybňující holocaust. Ovšem měla by to být výjimka jediná a měla by zůstat v gesci jednotlivých států.
4) V poslední době jsme svědky rozšíření násilí v zemích EU. Jedná se jednak o násilí způsobené teroristy s arabskými a africkými kořeny či jejich sympatizanty, jejichž pojítkem je vyznávání militantních verzí islámu a za druhé o násilí ze strany militantních demonstrantů z hnutí BLM a jejich sympatizantů. To první odsuzujeme a strůjce nejrůznějších atentátů a násilných činů se snažíme trestat. V tom druhém případě je ovšem násilí tolerováno. A to je velice špatná politika. Násilí je vždy jen násilí. A je zcela jedno z jakých pohnutek je pácháno. K žádnému druhu násilí nesmíme být tolerantní. Jen tak jej lze vymýtit jako celek.
5) Jsme svědky ničení kulturních památek, soch, historických artefaktů jako odplatu za dávné historické činy. Tyto projevy barbarství, násilí a netolerance však nemohou být omlouvány a nesmějí zůstat nepotrestány. Stejně tak jako nesmějí zůstat nepotrestány rasistické násilné trestné činy ať už jsou páchány kýmkoli bez rozdílu barvy pleti. Bohužel, zločiny páchané našimi evropskými spoluobčany tmavé pleti jsou často omlouvány, bagatelizovány a tolerovány. To je však nejjistější cesta k rozdělení společnosti, nárůstu extremismu a k postupné radikalizaci většinové společnosti. Výsledkem může, v extrémním případě, být rasová "válka" bílých proti černým. A kdo zvítězí je dopředu jasné. Domnívám se, že toto není cílem Evropské komise a není to ani cíl jednotlivých národních vlád, ale ani žádného z drtivé většiny rozumných občanů Evropské Unie jako celku. Pokud se však většinová společnost bude cítit i nadále diskriminována a ostrakizována ze strany Evropské komise a dalších orgánů Evropské Unie, situace se může podstatně zhoršit a ani výše uvedený výsledek není nemožný. A chtěl bych v tomto směru varovat, že trpělivost většinové společnosti a její tolerance je v posledních letech zkoušena až příliš a dlouho již nevydrží.
6) Vzhledem k výše uvedenému považuji nápad zařadit verbální projevy nenávisti či rasismu pod článek 83 jako nepromyšlený a hloupý. Evropská komise by neměla jakýmkoli způsobem omezovat svobodu projevu. Dokonce ani toho nenávistného nebo rasistického. Proč? Protože je vždy lepší když si tito lidé vybijí svoji agresi, nenávist, pocit frustrace či zklamání verbálně na sociálních sítích, či kdekoli jinde než aby se v nich hromadila a časem si ji vybili formou fyzického násilí. Protože tam přesně to bude směřovat. A výsledkem bude podpora extremistických stran a hnutí, vznik soukromých ozbrojených skupin, nejrůznějších domobran, nárůst násilí, netolerance, strachu a nepochopení.
7) Evropská komise nesmí zasahovat do svobody vyjadřování. Už nyní je v očích evropských občanů extrémně nepopulární, už nyní je bruselská administrativy obyčejnými lidmi nenáviděna napříč jednotlivými státy, už nyní si nadpoloviční občanů EU myslí, že více škodí než prospívá. Evropská komise nemá aktivisticky zasahovat do práv jednotlivých států, a to ani v případě, že se jí něco nelíbí. Proč? Za prvé proto, že nemá v rukou moc na to aby cokoli skutečně reálně změnila. A za druhé proto, že svým aktivismem může způsobit jediné - a tím je rozpad Evropské Unie jako celku.

S pozdravem

Dipl. Ing. Miloš Sivák

Karel Šebesta

Date:2021/04/17 17:27:46 Country:CZE EU_CITIZEN WITHINFO

To put it bluntly, this is another case where the EU is breaching its own principle of subsidiarity to enact legislation that nobody has asked for. It's a very thinly veiled power grab of member state responsibilities justified by a flimsy excuse designed to strenghten the role of the EU at the expense of member states. 99,9% of hate crimes occur between citizens of the same state, therefore their international consequences are as close to zero as they can get. There is no reason why the EU should have a say in how those hate crimes are punished nor in what the states deem to be hate speech and what is just "speech". There is also no reason why "hate speech" (itself a very flimsy concept whose dangerousness is vastly overstated for political gain) should be considered as dangerous as terrorism, human trafficking and other serious crimes.

Furthermore, the idea that inviduals should have no say in this but NGOs and other entities which overstate the dangerousness of hate speech for their own gain is preposterous and makes it quite clear who exactly is pushing for this and that this is another attempt to get the power of decision making as far as possible from the common voter since once enacted, it would take a Europe-wide initiative to reverse. I'm not even going to go into how anti-free-speech this proposal is.

I'm completely opposed to this legislation, roadmap and the whole direction this proposal stands for.

Isabela MIHALACHE

Date:2021/04/16 16:01:19 Country:BEL NGO WITHINFO

The European Roma Grassroot Organisation Network (ERGO Network) welcomes the initiative of the European Commission to include hate speech and hate crime on the grounds of sex, sexual orientation, age and disability in the list of EU crimes in Article 83(1) TFEU.
Despite lack of systematic monitoring and collection of data on hate speech and hate crime on the grounds of sex, sexual orientation, age and disability, existing reports, suggest that LGBTI people and persons with disabilities suffer disproportionately from hate crime, hate speech and violence.
However, currently, the EU law does not require its Member States to recognise sexual orientation, gender identity and/or gender expression as a bias motivating factor in criminal law. The EU only recognises racism and xenophobia in its 2008 Framework decision on combating racism and xenophobia by means of criminal law. However, under EU law, age, disability, gender, gender identity, sexual orientation and social or economic status are protected characteristics. Article 21 of the Charter of Fundamental Rights of the European Union prohibits discrimination based on an open-ended list of grounds, including, disability, sexual orientation and social origin. In addition, the EU’s Directive on the rights of victims recognises that the nature of bias crimes and the victim’s personal characteristics, including sexual orientation, gender identity and gender expression, must be considered together with the type or nature and the circumstances of the crime to determine whether it is a hate crime, a bias crime or a crime committed with a discriminatory motive.
Due to these legislative gaps, an attack against a transexual woman who is targeted with homophobic slurs and subsequently beaten up on the street will be recorded, investigated, and prosecuted as a common crime. A similar attack suffered by a black woman and associated with racist slurs can be recorded, investigated and prosecuted as a hate crime.
The adoption of EU laws addressing hate crimes motivated on these and other grounds would bridge the protection gap at the national level, by obliging all Member States to investigate and prosecute incitement to violence or hatred on grounds such disability, gender identity or sexual orientation as criminal offences. It should also further enable law enforcement and judicial authorities to also record, investigate and prosecute hate speech and hate crime from multiple or intersecting grounds, especially in the case of Roma and LGBTI persons, who are routinely subject to hate speech and hate crimes. Existing data shows that hate crime laws and practices view and address protected categories as single and separated entities. For examples, police officers tend to choose only category of hate speech or hate crime, often based on their lack understanding of how to investigate the intersectionality of hate crimes or using the victim’s perception of the crime or their own perception of the victim’s (affected) identity- especially if the victim is a trans woman.
ERGO Network calls on the EU to extend the list of EU crimes to cover hate speech and hate crime to cover the grounds of disability, age, gender and sexual orientation and involve LGBTI persons, elderly and persons with disability in the design, development, implementation and monitoring of initiatives related to combatting hate speech and hate crime as well as improve ways, tools and mechanisms for monitoring, reporting and collecting data disaggregated by bias motivation, included by sex, sexual orientation, age and disability. Further guidelines should be developed for Member States on how to employ an intersectional approach in legislation and state responses to hate speech and hate crime.

Date:2021/04/15 20:15:01 Country:IRL EU_CITIZEN ANONYMOUS

It is of course immensely sad that EU citizens of various faiths and genders are subject to intolerant attitudes and objectionable viewpoints. That said, there is little of merit in any proposal to further criminalize viewpoints, and this proposal is at base a series of massive non sequiturs cloaked in virtue.

The EU does not have harmonious laws on hate speech for several obvious reasons. a) Defining hate is near impossible, because it is a spongy and contested word -- it can denote incitement to violence or it can mean words that I subjectively hold to be "violence" or "harmful". b) Different member states have differing historical contexts on the matter of censorship, which is what we are actually discussing: a proposal for censorship. In my member state, the Church was once fond of intrusion into the minds of people; elsewhere fascists and communists tried their ideological hand. Authoritarians after all are always morally pure and thus love to tell people what they can and can't say. c) Who decides? The EU might set up a body of well-meaning bureaucrats, well-meaning academics, or well-intentioned retirees from the judiciary. But it is not in the gift of the bureaucracy to tell me what to read, think or say. I will defy them. d) Who would we ban first? It's a serious question: remember hate has no ostensive meaning. It is just stuff I don't like. So, who's hateful? Neo-classical economists? Marxists? Aren't they hateful extremists? How many people were sent to gulags because some radical read Kapital and decided to mix things up?

So, here's the first problem: lots of things are bad, objectionable, but it does not follow that we should criminalise things we don't like. EU member states have different legislation that nests in different histories and cultures, but it does not follow that we should all pretend that doesn't matter.

We also have a massive social-scientific problem at the heart of this proposal. The problem is specified as increased frequency of hate crimes in a long-run context in which violent crime in general (and indeed terrorism) is declining -- spikes are not vectors. Moreover, the proposal opens with the proposition that, "Available research and surveys provide indications about the extent and the increase of hate crime and hate speech (including online), as well as the links between the two and their impact on victims and on society at large." This would not get your through first year sociology in a regional university. What do we mean by indications? Crime and speech are conflated (note: they're not the same thing). And where is the robust evidence of direct causation between speech and crime? The only robust peer-reviewed study cited is a very loose discussion of correlation between Twitter statements and real-world crime, independent of "trigger events". I'm sure the authors mean well, but this is not sufficient evidence upon which to base policy. I would suggest the research and evidence required to support this is lacking, and I would be less than convinced by the academic ability of many of the individuals and think tanks that swarmed into these iffy areas of research.

Contrary to the canalised presentation of the initiative, as a matter of fact the need for a common criminal law response at EU level has not been demonstrated. We have already seen a significant exit from the EU, citing in part a creeping authoritarian attitude. The surest route to bigger problems is to continue to believe that everyone should think the same.

The consultative process that follows is to reach out to equality bodies and the usual cast of NGO characters and academics who depend on terminological inexactitude for a living. My recommendation is that the public be consulted more widely. It is, after all, their thoughts that the initiative is aiming to police.

Date:2021/04/15 18:34:04 Country:CAN NON_EU_CITIZEN ANONYMOUS

I am a woman and a lesbian, and I strongly oppose the proposed inclusion of “hate speech” in the list of EU crimes in Article 83(1) TFEU.

Changes happen by reaching out, listening to and communicating with people holding a different opinion, not by punishing people for “wrongthink”.

Marine Uldry

Date:2021/04/09 10:42:31 Country:BEL NGO WITHINFO

The European Disability Forum welcomes the commitment of the European Commission to protect the rights of victims in the European Union (EU) and the initiative to extend the list of EU crimes in Article 83(1) of the Treaty on the Functioning of the European Union (TFEU) to hate speech and hate crime.

Similar to other discriminated persons in the EU, persons with disabilities are at higher risk of being victims of hate speech and hate crime. In general, persons with disabilities, including women with disabilities, and persons with intellectual and psychosocial disabilities, are at higher risk of harassment, violence and crime, including within the digital world. According to data published by the Fundamental Rights Agency of the European Union (FRA), 50% of persons with disabilities have reported being harassed over a 5-year period, comparing to 37% of persons without disabilities.

Yet, disability related hate speech and hate crime are not recognised and sanction as such by all EU Member States. In addition, persons with disabilities continue to face barriers in reporting offences due to a multitude of barriers in the justice system. This leads to lower reporting and limited data on hate crimes and hate speech faced by persons with disabilities in the EU.

EDF calls on the EU to extend the list of EU crimes to cover hate speech and hate crime beyond the grounds covered by Framework Decision 2008/913/JHA. The list of EU crimes and EU legislation and initiatives to combat hate speech and hate crime should cover the grounds of disability, age, gender and sexual orientation (in line with list of discrimination grounds under Article 19 TFEU).

This will contribute to the implementation of the UN Convention on the Rights of Persons with Disabilities (CRPD) ratified by the EU and all its member states and the Disability Rights Strategy 2021-2030 and Victims’ Rights Strategy 2020-2025. Article 16 of the CRPD requires parties to the Convention “to take all legislative, administrative, social, education and other measures to protect persons with disabilities, both within and outside the home, from all forms of exploitation, violence and abuse, including their gender-based aspects.”

More specifically we recommend that the EU:

• Involve representative organisations of persons with disabilities
- In the design, development, implementation and monitoring of initiatives related to combatting hate speech and hate crime
- In the design, delivery, and monitoring of victim support services
• Extend the list of EU crimes to cover hate speech and hate crime and cover the grounds of disability, age, gender (in line with the list of discrimination grounds under Article 19 TEFU).
• Ensure that minimum rules on the definition of criminal offences and sanctions in the areas of hate speech and hate crime take into account bias based on disability, including the digital domain, and that the disability of the victims never allows for lighter sentences for the perpetrator.
• Take measures to improve the rights of victims with disabilities, including to ensure they can adequately report the offence or crime to national authorities (in light of EDF recommendations on the EU Strategy on Victims’ Rights 2020-2024), and that they receive accessibility and inclusive support. Expansion of third-party reporting mechanisms could also be considered.
• Support the creation by Member States of mandatory training on disability rights for all law enforcement and criminal justice system officials.
• Collect data on hate speech and hate crime, including in online environments, disaggregated by the motivation bias (including disability-bias), and the victims’ disability, gender and age.

Our full submission is attached.

Peter Wieczorek

Chara Bakalis

Date:2021/03/30 15:48:52 Country:GBR ACADEMIC_RESEARCH_INSTITTUTION WITHINFO

My name is Chara Bakalis, and I am a Principal Lecturer in Law at Oxford Brookes University in the UK. My area of expertise is hate crime and hate speech with a particular focus on online hate speech.


I have several articles I have written which you may find useful. However, I can only attach one file to this response so please email me if you would like me to send you the others.

1) Whether hate speech and hate crime on the grounds of sex, sexual orientation, age and disability are
particularly serious crimes, taking into account, amongst others, their effects on the victims and on society as a
whole;

I think it is important to recognise the particular harm caused by hate crime. Whilst much emphasis has (rightly) been placed on the idea that hate crimes 'hurt more', it is also important to recognise the ways in which hate crimes damage broader societal values, namely that of equality. There is a clear link between anti-discrimination initiatives and hate crime. Given the EU's commitment to anti-discrimination, it makes sense to extend this to the criminal law. (Article attached)

2) Whether hate speech and hate crime on the grounds of sex, sexual orientation, age and disability have a
cross-border dimension resulting from the nature or impact of such offences or from a special need to combat
them on a common basis, taking into account, amongst others, the possible links between the two offences as
well as any effects that may spill over from one Member State to the other;

This is particularly relevant in relation to online hate speech, where the proliferation of hate on the internet means that traditional forms of policing cannot cope with the sheer volume of material which could be classified as illegal. As such, more emphasis needs to be placed on social media companies to take responsibility for the material that appears on their platforms. Whilst some EU countries have already tried to take action (such as Germany), it will be much easier to make the social media companies take seriously their role as moderators if there is a joint response from the EU. (Article available - please email me if you would like a copy cbakalis@brookes.ac.uk)


3) Whether there are developments in crime that justify the inclusion of hate speech and hate crime on the
grounds of sex, sexual orientation, age and disability in the list of EU crimes in Article 83(1) TFEU.


In the UK, where there is an established framework of hate crime legislation that is implemented well by the police, there are x prosecutions for hate crime every year. The scale of the problem is obvious and these numbers can be used to lay bare the broader problems of racism/misogyny/homophobia etc in society. Whilst clearly not a panacea, there are certainly benefits in establishing and using hate crime legislation, beyond those which accrue to individual victims.

This is a debate that is being played out currently in England and Wales in relation to whether gender/sex should be added to hate crime legislation. One real benefit of adding gender is to highlight the high level of gender-based violence that exists in society. Hate crime provides a mechanism for forcing the police to take notice of this kind of violence and to put in place initiatives to alleviate it. (Article available - please email me if you would like a copy cbakalis@brookes.ac.uk)

Potrebbe essere utile, infine, creare una piattaforma, a livello dell'UE, così da consentire alle possibili parti interessate, di segnalare i comportamenti anomali (possibili crimini d'odio), impegnandosi a creare così una sorta di collaborazione tra governi locali, per la disamina dei presunti crimini e per le "punizioni" da comminare, uniformemente.

Helen de Mel

Date:2021/03/15 11:12:31 Country:FIN NGO WITHINFO

Assessment of the call
The NCTN meets the criteria of both a civic organisation and, more relevantly, an expert network with extensive knowledge of certain areas of hate crime and radicalisation.

The most relevant expertise for the NCTN would appear to be Islamist radicalism, which is relevant especially for the areas of antisemitism and gendered violence, as well as hate crime towards sexual minorities.
Secondary expertise includes polarising phenomena in society at large. Though not explicitly stated, the consultation should warrant a critical assessment of the trends pertaining to left-wing extremist hate crimes.
When reflected upon the stated aims of the call, both of these areas appear to represent a certain niche expertise that makes them competitive in this particular call.

Of particular importance is the cross-discipline operational expertise of the NCTN in collaboration with the IACSP and other relevant strategic partners. The ability to extend research beyond the limits of a purely academic scope offers a distinct competitive edge for this call.
As there is a large body of academic knowledge pertaining to the most obvious problems addressed by the call, it would appear prudent to focus on the less obvious niches pertaining to the phenomena the Commission seeks to address.
Proposal for action

Based on the assessment above, it is proposed that the NCTN participate in the consultations by addressing specific niches where the expertise of the NCTN provides a distinctive competitive edge by:

1) assessing the threat of hate crime against women, sexual minorities and persons of the Jewish persuasion originating in radicalised and politicised Islamic movements;

2) assessing the incidence, prevalence as well as potential for domestic terrorism in the sphere of hate crime originating in left-wing radicalism;

3) assessing the impact on fragmentation and polarisation of society caused by the proposed measures; and

4) assessing the adaptive mechanism whereby radicalised groups may seek to benefit from or mitigate the aims of the proposed measures.

In carrying out said assessments the key strengths of the NCTN relate to its cross-discipline approach and access to intelligence-based data about the subjects. These strengths may enable the NCTN to challenge key assumptions limiting the utility of purely academic research, in a way that may provide entirely new insights on the subject matter.
As to the methods that appear most promising, the integration of operational intelligence with academic methods seems a promising avenue of approach. This enables both the aforementioned challenging of key assumptions as well as qualitative identification of new phenomena and trends. This may then be utilised further to, by quantitative means, measure the incidence and prevalence of (potential) hate crimes, e.g., by means of victimisation surveys, possibly in populations not previously identified as actual or potential victims of hate crime.

The concept of “intelligence led research” should be seen as a distinctive competitive advantage that few organisations the size of the NCTN may offer and could be emphasised when submitting proposals for action. This kind of analysis and insight has traditionally been the exclusive domain of authorities that may be both overworked, subject to heavy regulation and politically influenced.

Turku 9.3.2021

Distribution Andrin Raj
a.raj@iacsp-nctn.org
Director
Nordic Counter Terrorism Network
Helsinki, Finland

There is a need for EU level platform (e.g. website) so all stakeholders meaning all people who are citizens of the EU, residents of the EU or those who are travelling the EU be able to report the hate crime. In the first phase the platform will be connected to the local government body in the country where crime took place and they will receive the track number and date required to address the issue (this date will be created based on number of reports and their complexity ) and in case this did not addressed in the timely manner, it will be sent to the supervisory body like inspection office automatically and in case it is not address it will be sent to the EU body. In case issue have been addressed , there would be survey and ask the victims about what they think about the action and in case of the dissatisfaction, it will be sent to the supervisory body. The website should be transparent and show the number of reports and their status. In one part we shall let the NGO to get engaged and collaborate with the local governments to solve the issue and regularly look into crimes and punishments and improving them. There is a need to find the things where causing the crime to take into the place and work on resolving the conflict and fixing them.

Date:2021/02/24 12:16:40 Country:ROU EU_CITIZEN WITHINFO

I don't think there is any need for separating crimes for hate or other type of crime; assault on people is a crime regardless of the reason and should be treated according to existing laws.
Also speech should not be regulated in any case by the government, it is an attack on free speech. Classifying some type of words as hate speech will just open the window for banning other words and ideas in the future, where will the limit be on hate speech? one can interpret anything someone says as hate speech if one wants to. I believe that hate speech should be resolved through cultural and social means and not legislation, and especially not through banning. Just the same, racism, sexism, xenophobia, etc. should be solved through social and cultural means and not through government involvement, the laws should treat crime the same regardless of the perpetrator or the victim.
